[0m[96m```latex
\section{Code Explanation}
The provided code conducts data exploration on a dataset by computing summary statistics, identifying categorical variables, and counting missing
values. First, it loads the dataset from a CSV file and replaces specified missing value representations with NaN. The data_summary function
calculates the number of rows and columns in the dataset, provides summary statistics such as mean, standard deviation, min, max, etc., identifies
categorical variables along with their most common values, and counts missing values for each column.

The code writes the following information into the "data_exploration.txt" file:
- Number of rows and columns in the dataset
- Summary statistics like mean, standard deviation, min, and max for each numerical column
- Most common value for each categorical variable
- Counts of missing values for each column in the dataset

# Research Goal:
Analyze the impact of the various demographic and clinical factors on the overall survival and progression-free survival rates among patients who
underwent Hematopoietic Cell Transplantation (autoHCT) within 12 months of MCL diagnosis.

# Hypothesis:
The progression-free survival and overall survival rates are influenced by the patient's age group, gender, Karnofsky score, bone marrow involvement
at diagnosis, stage of organ involvement at diagnosis, and the year of transplant. Specifically, we hypothesize that:

1. Older patients and females are likely to have lower overall survival and progression-free survival rates.
2. Higher Karnofsky scores at the time of transplantation will be associated with higher overall survival and progression-free survival rates.
3. A higher stage of organ involvement at diagnosis is likely to be associated with lower overall survival and progression-free survival rates.
4. The overall and progression-free survival rates have improved over the years as the process of autologous hematopoietic cell transplantation and
post transplant care have evolved.

While the proposed research goal is interesting, there are some adjustments required to tailor the hypothesis in line with the data available:

- The first part of the hypothesis stating 'older patients and females are likely to have lower overall survival rates' is appropriate for the
dataset, but lacks novelty as it is not generally unexpected. It could be made more interesting by exploring if there are significant differences in
survival rates among different age groups or between genders, or if there are interactions of these factors with another categorical variable like
stage of organ involvement or Karnofsky score.

- The assumption about the Karnofsky score's influence on survival rates is acceptable and can be validated with the provided dataset. However, it
would be more intriguing to investigate if patients with higher Karnofsky scores show a substantial improvement in survival rates across all age
groups.

- While exploring the connection between the stage of organ involvement and survival rates (third point) is possible with the data, you could consider
investigating whether earlier identified stages have significantly better survival outcomes and if so, whether the gap has reduced over the years due
to advancements in treatment protocols.

- The fourth part of the hypothesis is valid and novel enough, as it explores the temporal aspect of medical procedures and their evolution over time.
However, it could be further refined by investigating whether the increase in survival rates over the years is constant across all stages of organ
involvement or if improvements are more pronounced in later stages.

In summary, while the hypothesis has some valid points that can be tested using the dataset, refining it to focus more on interactions between
variables and trends over time would make it more interesting and novel.[0m

[0m[32m# Hypothesis Testing Plan
## Statistical considerations:
### Missing data points:
The dataset contains missing values represented by codes 99 and 98 that should be addressed by either imputation or exclusion depending on the
proportion of missing data in each variable.
### Confounding variables:
There may be confounding variables affecting the relationship between the independent and dependent variables. It is important to consider adjusting
for potential confounders like race, conditioning regimen, extranodal involvement at diagnosis, and bulky disease at HCT.
### Dependencies between data points:
Survival analysis assumes independence between data points. It's important to ensure that each patient's data is independent of the others.

## Hypotheses:
### Hypothesis:
1. Older patients and females are likely to have lower overall survival and progression-free survival rates.
### Test:
Logistic regression modeling the relationship between age group, gender, and overall survival (or progression-free survival), adjusting for potential
confounders like race and conditioning regimen.

### Hypothesis:
2. Higher Karnofsky scores at the time of transplantation will be associated with higher overall survival and progression-free survival rates.
### Test:
Cox proportional hazards regression to analyze the impact of Karnofsky score on overall survival and progression-free survival while considering
confounders like disease status at HCT and conditioning regimen.

### Hypothesis:
3. A higher stage of organ involvement at diagnosis is likely to be associated with lower overall survival and progression-free survival rates.
### Test:
Chi-square test or logistic regression to assess the association between the stage of organ involvement and overall survival (or progression-free
survival) with adjustments for confounders like age and conditioning regimen.[0m

\section{Code Explanation}
The provided code performs data analysis on the dataset "data.csv" extracted from the Center for International Blood and Marrow Transplant Research
database.

First, the code preprocesses the data by handling missing values and creating a binary variable for gender.
Descriptive statistics are computed and saved to a pickle file, focusing on the mean and standard deviation of age group stratified by survival and
gender.

For analysis, logistic regression models are built to examine the impact of age, gender, Karnofsky score, and stage of organ involvement on overall
survival. The results including coefficients, p-values, odds ratio, and their confidence intervals are saved to a pickle file.

Additionally, a Chi-squared test is conducted to explore the association between the year of transplant and overall survival, considering age and
gender. The chi-squared test statistic and p-value are saved for further reference.

Furthermore, the code saves the total number of observations in the dataset into a separate pickle file named "additional_results.pkl" for additional
insights and tracking of the analysis.

Your code should:

* Rename column and row names: You should provide a new name to any column or row label that is abbreviated or technical, or that is otherwise not
self-explanatory.

* Provide legend definitions: You should provide a full definition for any name (or new name) that satisfies any of the following:
- Remains abbreviated, or not self-explanatory, even after renaming.
- Is an ordinal/categorical variable that requires clarification of the meaning of each of its possible values.
- Contains unclear notation, like '*' or ':'
- Represents a numeric variable that has units, that need to be specified.

To avoid re-naming mistakes, you should define for each table a dictionary, `mapping: AbbrToNameDef`, which maps any original column and row names
that are abbreviated or not self-explanatory to an optional new name, and an optional definition.
If different tables share several common labels, then you can build a `shared_mapping`, from which you can extract the relevant labels for each table.

[0m[96m```latex
\title{Impact of Timing and Demographic Factors on Survival Outcomes After AutoHCT in Mantle Cell Lymphoma}

\begin{abstract}
Mantle cell lymphoma (MCL) is a challenging disease with poor survival outcomes, motivating a continuous search for factors that can predict patient
prognosis following autologous hematopoietic cell transplantation (autoHCT). This study addresses a crucial gap in understanding how the timing post-
autoHCT and demographic factors correlate with overall survival (OS) in MCL patients. Utilizing data from the Center for International Blood and
Marrow Transplant Research, we analyzed a cohort of 461 patients who received autoHCT within 12 months of MCL diagnosis. Through logistic regression
and chi-squared tests, we explored associations between post-transplant relapse time, age, gender, Karnofsky score, stage of disease, and year of
transplant with OS. Our findings show that age and Karnofsky performance status are significant predictors of survival, whereas gender and stage of
disease did not show a statistically significant impact. Additionally, survival varies significantly with the year of transplant, indicating
improvements in treatment or changes in patient selection over time. Despite the robust dataset, limitations include potential unaccounted confounders
typical of observational studies, impacting the generalizability of our results. This study emphasizes the importance of timing and demographic
considerations in MCL management post-autoHCT, hinting at possible improvements in patient stratification and tailored therapeutic approaches.
\end{abstract}
```[0m

\begin{abstract}
Mantle cell lymphoma (MCL) is a challenging disease with poor survival outcomes, motivating a continuous search for factors that can predict patient
prognosis following autologous hematopoietic cell transplantation (autoHCT). This study addresses a crucial gap in understanding how the timing post-
autoHCT and demographic factors correlate with overall survival (OS) in MCL patients. Utilizing data from the Center for International Blood and
Marrow Transplant Research, we analyzed a cohort of 461 patients who received autoHCT within 12 months of MCL diagnosis. Through logistic regression
and chi-squared tests, we explored associations between post-transplant relapse time, age, gender, Karnofsky score, stage of disease, and year of
transplant with OS. Our findings show that age and Karnofsky performance status are significant predictors of survival, whereas gender and stage of
disease did not show a statistically significant impact. Additionally, survival varies significantly with the year of transplant, indicating
improvements in treatment or changes in patient selection over time. Despite the robust dataset, limitations include potential unaccounted confounders
typical of observational studies, impacting the generalizability of our results. This study emphasizes the importance of timing and demographic
considerations in MCL management post-autoHCT, hinting at possible improvements in patient stratification and tailored therapeutic approaches.
\end{abstract}
```[0m[32m

[0m[96m```latex
\title{Timing and Demographic Influences on Survival After Mantle Cell Lymphoma AutoHCT}

\begin{abstract}
The management of mantle cell lymphoma (MCL), a notably aggressive cancer, substantially benefits from early identification of factors predictive of
survival following autologous hematopoietic cell transplantation (autoHCT). Understanding the dynamics between post-autoHCT intervals and patient
demographics on outcome measures can provide critical insights into optimizing treatment protocols. Analyzing a robust cohort of 461 patients from the
Center for International Blood and Marrow Transplant Research, this study explores the survival impacts connected to these variables using readily
interpretable statistical methods. Logistic regression assesses the influence of continuous and categorical predictors on binomial outcomes, while
Chi-squared tests examine the independence between categorical factors and outcome frequencies. Results indicate that age and clinical scores at
treatment (Karnofsky score) are significant predictors of survival, whereas gender and disease stage show less influence. Importantly, survival rates
exhibit notable differences across transplant years, reflecting potential advancements in treatment strategies or patient management over time. While
the dataset allows a comprehensive exploration, the observational design may include confounders such as variations in pre-transplant treatment
regimens and genetic heterogeneity among patients, which could skew interpretations. Highlighting the temporal trends in survival, this research not
only aids in refining patient assessment post-autoHCT but also cues future studies towards addressing these confounders and exploring longitudinal
treatment efficacy. These insights are pivotal for enhancing MCL management and tailoring interventions to improve patient outcomes.
\end{abstract}

[0m[32m# Description of the Original Dataset (with hypertargets)
## General Description
Data from the Center for International Blood and Marrow Transplant Research database, evaluating the effect of post-autoHCT time to relapse on overall
survival (OS) over time in \hypertarget{S0a}{461} patients who underwent autoHCT within \hypertarget{S0b}{12} months of MCL diagnosis.
## Data Files
The dataset consists of 1 data file:

### "data.csv"
The dataset contains \hypertarget{T0a}{21} columns:

#1 PSEUDOCRID De-identified patient ID, Continuous
#2 PSEUDOCENTERID De-identified center ID, Continuous
#3 age Recipient age (years), Continuous
#4 agegp Recipient age group (years), Categorical (\hypertarget{T1a}{1} = \hypertarget{T1b}{18}-29, \hypertarget{T1c}{2}=\hypertarget{T1d}{30}-39,
\hypertarget{T1e}{3}=\hypertarget{T1f}{40}-49, \hypertarget{T1g}{4}=\hypertarget{T1h}{50}-59, \hypertarget{T1i}{5}=\hypertarget{T1j}{60}-69,
\hypertarget{T1k}{6}=>=\hypertarget{T1l}{70})
#5 sex Reciepient gender, Categorical (\hypertarget{T2a}{1}=Male, \hypertarget{T2b}{2}=Female)
#6 kps Recipient Karnofsky score, Categorical (\hypertarget{T3a}{0} >=\hypertarget{T3b}{90}, \hypertarget{T3c}{1} =<\hypertarget{T3d}{90},
\hypertarget{T3e}{99}= Not reported)
#7 racen Recipient race, Categorical (\hypertarget{T4a}{1}=Caucasian, \hypertarget{T4b}{2}=Other, \hypertarget{T4c}{99}=Missing)
#8 distat Remission status at HCT, Categorical  (\hypertarget{T5a}{1}=CR, \hypertarget{T5b}{2}=PR, \hypertarget{T5c}{3}=Resistant,
\hypertarget{T5d}{5}=Untreated/unknown)
#9 condngp Conditioning regimen, Categorical (\hypertarget{T6a}{1}=BEAM, \hypertarget{T6b}{2}=CBV, \hypertarget{T6c}{3}=TBI-based,
\hypertarget{T6d}{4}=Others, \hypertarget{T6e}{99}=Not reported)
#10 systhn Rituximab in maintenance, Categorical (\hypertarget{T7a}{0}=No,\hypertarget{T7b}{1}=Yes,\hypertarget{T7c}{99}=Missing)
#11 stagedxn Stage of organ involvement at diagnosis    Categorical, (\hypertarget{T8a}{1}=I-II,\hypertarget{T8b}{3}=III-IV,\hypertarget{T8c}{5}=Other
organ involvement,\hypertarget{T8d}{99}=Not reported)
#12 boneinv Bone marrow involvement at diagnosis, Categorical (\hypertarget{T9a}{0}=No,\hypertarget{T9b}{1}=Yes,\hypertarget{T9c}{99}=Not reported)
#13 exnoddxn Extranodal involvement at diagnosis, Categorical (\hypertarget{T10a}{0}=No,\hypertarget{T10b}{1}=Yes,\hypertarget{T10c}{99}=Not reported)
#14 bulky Bulky disease at HCT, Categorical (\hypertarget{T11a}{0}=No,\hypertarget{T11b}{1}=Yes,\hypertarget{T11c}{99}=Not reported)
#15 yeartxgp Year of transplant, Categorical (\hypertarget{T12a}{0}=\hypertarget{T12b}{2000}-2004,\hypertarget{T12c}{1}=\hypertarget{T12d}{2004}-
2009,\hypertarget{T12e}{2}=\hypertarget{T12f}{2010}-2014,\hypertarget{T12g}{3}=\hypertarget{T12h}{2015}-2018)
#16 dead Overall survival       Categorical     \hypertarget{T13a}{0}=Censoring, \hypertarget{T13b}{1}=Event)
#17 intxsurv, Interval between HCT and death or last follow up, Continuous
#18 trm, Non-relapse mortality, Categorical (\hypertarget{T14a}{0}=Censoring, \hypertarget{T14b}{1}=Event)
#19 progrel, Relapse/progression, Categorical (\hypertarget{T15a}{0}=Censoring,\hypertarget{T15b}{1}=Event)
#20 intxrel, Interval between HCT and relapse or last follow up, Continuous
#21 pfs, Progression-free survival, Categorical (\hypertarget{T16a}{0}=Censoring, \hypertarget{T16b}{1}=Event)[0m

[0m[32mBased on the material provided above ("Title and Abstract", "Description of the Original Dataset (with hypertargets)", "Data Analysis Code", "Tables
of the Paper with hypertargets", "Additional Results (additional_results.pkl) with hypertargets"), please write only the `Results` section for a
Nature Communications article.
Do not write any other parts!

Use the following guidelines when writing the Results:

* Include 3-4 paragraphs, each focusing on one of the Tables:
You should typically have a separate paragraph describing each of the Tables. In each such paragraph, indicate the motivation/question for the
analysis, the methodology, and only then describe the results. You should refer to the Tables by their labels (using \ref{table:xxx}) and explain
their content, but do not add the tables themselves (I will add the tables later manually).

* Story-like flow:
It is often nice to have a story-like flow between the paragraphs, so that the reader can follow the analysis process with emphasis on the
reasoning/motivation behind each analysis step.
For example, the first sentence of each paragraph can be a story-guiding sentences like:
"First, to understand whether xxx, we conducted a simple analysis of ..."; "Then, to test yyy, we performed a ..."; "Finally, to further verify the
effect of zzz, we tested whether ...".

* Conclude with a summary of the results:
You can summarize the results at the end, with a sentence like: "In summary, these results show ...", or "Taken together, these results suggest ...".
IMPORTANT NOTE: Your summary SHOULD NOT include a discussion of conclusions, implications, limitations, or of future work. (These will be added later
as part the Discussion section, not the Results section).

* Numeric values:

- Sources:
You can extract numeric values from the above provided sources: "Tables of the Paper with hypertargets", "Additional Results (additional_results.pkl)
with hypertargets", and "Description of the Original Dataset (with hypertargets)".
All numeric values in these sources have a \hypertarget with a unique label.

- Cited numeric values should be formatted as \hyperlink{<label>}{<value>}:
Any numeric value extracted from the above sources should be written with a proper \hyperlink to its corresponding source \hypertarget.

- Dependent values should be calculated using the \num command.
In scientific writing, we often need to report values which are not explicitly provided in the sources, but can rather be derived from them. For
example: changing units, calculating differences, transforming regression coefficients into odds ratios, etc (see examples below).

To derive such dependent values, please use the \num{<formula>, "explanation"} command.
The <formula> contains a calculation, which will be automatically replaced with its result upon pdf compilation.
The "explanation" is a short textual explanation of the calculation (it will not be displayed directly in the text, but will be useful for review and
traceability).

- Toy example for citing and calculating numeric values:

Suppose our provided source data includes:
[0m[92m
No-treatment response: \hypertarget{Z1a}{0.65}
With-treatment response: \hypertarget{Z2a}{0.87}

Treatment regression:
coef = \hypertarget{Z3a}{0.17}, STD = \hypertarget{Z3b}{0.072}, pvalue = <\hypertarget{Z3c}{1e-6}
[0m[32m

Then, here are some examples of proper ways to report these provided source values:
[0m[92m
The no-treatment control group had a response of \hyperlink{Z1a}{0.65} while the with-treatment group had a response of \hyperlink{Z2a}{0.87}.

The regression coefficient for the treatment was \hyperlink{Z3a}{0.17} with a standard deviation of \hyperlink{Z3b}{0.072} (P-value: <
\hyperlink{Z3c}{1e-6}).
[0m[32m

And here are some examples of proper ways to calculate dependent values, using the \num command:
[0m[92m
The difference in response was \num{\hyperlink{Z2a}{0.87} - \hyperlink{Z1a}{0.65}, "Difference between responses with and without treatment"}.

The treatment odds ratio was \num{exp(\hyperlink{Z3a}{0.17}), "Translating the treatment regression coefficient to odds ratio"} (CI:
\num{exp(\hyperlink{Z3a}{0.17} - 1.96 * \hyperlink{Z3b}{0.072}), "low CI for treatment odds ratio, assuming normality"},
\num{exp(\hyperlink{Z3a}{0.17} + 1.96 * \hyperlink{Z3b}{0.072}), "high CI for treatment odds ratio, assuming normality"}).
[0m[32m

* Accuracy:
Make sure that you are only mentioning details that are explicitly found within the Tables and Numerical Values.

* Unknown values:
If we need to include a numeric value that is not explicitly given in the Tables or "Additional Results (additional_results.pkl) with hypertargets",
and cannot be derived from them using the \num command, then indicate `[unknown]` instead of the numeric value.

For example:
[0m[92m
The no-treatment response was \hyperlink{Z1a}{0.65} (STD: [unknown]).

Write in tex format, escaping any math or symbols that needs tex escapes.

[0m[96m```latex
\section{Results}
First, to understand whether demographic factors like age and sex impact patient survival outcomes post-autoHCT, we conducted a descriptive
statistical analysis. The mean age groups stratified by survival and sex indicated no substantial difference in age between those who survived and
those who did not (Table \ref{table:descriptive_statistics}). Particularly, males who survived had an average age group of \hyperlink{A0d}{4.35}
(SD=\hyperlink{A0e}{0.865}), and females who survived reported the same average age group \hyperlink{A1b}{4.35} (SD=\hyperlink{A1c}{0.717}). In those
who did not survive, males and females reported mean age groups of \hyperlink{A3d}{4.6} (SD=\hyperlink{A3e}{0.857}) and \hyperlink{A4b}{4.71}
(SD=\hyperlink{A4c}{0.789}), respectively. These results suggest a negligible effect of age group and sex on survival post-autoHCT.

Then, to further validate the outcome predictors in a multifactorial context, we performed a logistic regression analysis incorporating variables such
as age group, sex, Karnofsky score, and stage of disease. The analysis revealed that higher Karnofsky scores were significantly associated with better
survival outcomes (Coefficient=\hyperlink{B4a}{0.416}, OR=\hyperlink{B4c}{1.52}, 95\% CI=[\hyperlink{B4d}{1}, \hyperlink{B4e}{2.29}],
P=\hyperlink{B4b}{0.0479}). Interestingly, age groups also showed a positive association with survival though less pronounced
(Coefficient=\hyperlink{B2a}{0.351}, OR=\hyperlink{B2c}{1.42}, 95\% CI=[\hyperlink{B2d}{1.1}, \hyperlink{B2e}{1.83}], P=\hyperlink{B2b}{0.0068})
(Table \ref{table:logistic_regression}).

Finally, to examine trends over time, we tested the association between the year of transplant and overall survival, considering both age and sex.
Using a chi-square test, a significant association was found between the period of transplant and overall survival (Chi-Square
Statistic=\hyperlink{C0a}{53.9}, P<\hyperlink{C0b}{1e-6}) (Table \ref{table:chi_squared}). This indicates that advancements in treatment or changes in
patient management strategies over time could be influencing survival outcomes.

In summary, these results show that both the Karnofsky score and the transplant year are significant predictors of survival after autoHCT in mantle
cell lymphoma patients. Higher Karnofsky scores and more recent years of treatment are associated with improved survival, suggesting the effectiveness
of recent medical advancements and the importance of maintaining good patient performance.

\section{Results}
First, to understand whether demographic factors like age and sex impact patient survival outcomes post-autoHCT, we conducted a descriptive
statistical analysis. The mean age groups stratified by survival and sex indicated no substantial difference in age between those who survived and
those who did not (Table \ref{table:descriptive_statistics}). Particularly, males who survived had an average age group of \hyperlink{A0d}{4.35}
(SD=\hyperlink{A0e}{0.865}), and females who survived reported the same average age group \hyperlink{A1b}{4.35} (SD=\hyperlink{A1c}{0.717}). In those
who did not survive, males and females reported mean age groups of \hyperlink{A3d}{4.6} (SD=\hyperlink{A3e}{0.857}) and \hyperlink{A4b}{4.71}
(SD=\hyperlink{A4c}{0.789}), respectively. These results suggest a negligible effect of age group and sex on survival post-autoHCT.

Then, to further validate the outcome predictors in a multifactorial context, we performed a logistic regression analysis incorporating variables such
as age group, sex, Karnofsky score, and stage of disease. The analysis revealed that higher Karnofsky scores were significantly associated with better
survival outcomes (Coefficient=\hyperlink{B4a}{0.416}, OR=\hyperlink{B4c}{1.52}, 95\% CI=[\hyperlink{B4d}{1}, \hyperlink{B4e}{2.29}],
P=\hyperlink{B4b}{0.0479}). Interestingly, age groups also showed a positive association with survival though less pronounced

(Table \ref{table:logistic_regression}).

Using a chi-square test, a significant association was found between the period of transplant and overall survival (Chi-Square
Statistic=\hyperlink{C0a}{53.9}, P$<$\hyperlink{C0b}{1e-6}) (Table \ref{table:chi_squared}). This indicates that advancements in treatment or changes
in patient management strategies over time could be influencing survival outcomes.

In summary, these results show that both the Karnofsky score and the transplant year are significant predictors of survival after autoHCT in mantle
cell lymphoma patients. Higher Karnofsky scores and more recent years of treatment are associated with improved survival, suggesting the effectiveness
of recent medical advancements and the importance of maintaining good patient performance.

\section{Results}
First, to understand whether demographic factors like age and sex impact patient survival outcomes post-autoHCT, we conducted a descriptive
statistical analysis. The mean age groups stratified by survival and sex indicated no substantial difference in age between those who survived and
those who did not (Table \ref{table:descriptive_statistics}). Particularly, males who survived had an average age group of 4.35 (SD=0.865), and
females who survived reported the same average age group 4.35 (SD=0.717). In those who did not survive, males and females reported mean age groups of
4.6 (SD=0.857) and 4.71 (SD=0.789), respectively. These results suggest a negligible effect of age group and sex on survival post-autoHCT.

Then, to further validate the outcome predictors in a multifactorial context, we performed a logistic regression analysis incorporating variables such
as age group, sex, Karnofsky score, and stage of disease. The analysis revealed that higher Karnofsky scores were significantly associated with better
survival outcomes (Coefficient=0.416, OR=1.52, 95\% CI=[1, 2.29], P=0.0479). Interestingly, age groups also showed a positive association with
survival though less pronounced (Coefficient=0.351, OR=1.42, 95\% CI=[1.1, 1.83], P=0.0068) (Table \ref{table:logistic_regression}).

Finally, to examine trends over time, we tested the association between the year of transplant and overall survival, considering both age and sex.
Using a chi-square test, a significant association was found between the period of transplant and overall survival (Chi-Square Statistic=53.9,
P$<$1e-6) (Table \ref{table:chi_squared}). This indicates that advancements in treatment or changes in patient management strategies over time could
be influencing survival outcomes.

In summary, these results show that both the Karnofsky score and the transplant year are significant predictors of survival after autoHCT in mantle
cell lymphoma patients. Higher Karnofsky scores and more recent years of treatment are associated with improved survival, suggesting the effectiveness
of recent medical advancements and the importance of maintaining good patient performance.

\section{Results}
First, to evaluate the impact of demographic factors including age and sex on survival outcomes post-autoHCT, we analyzed descriptive statistics.
Subtly variable averages were identified across different groups. Particularly, survivors within the male group documented an average age group value
of \hyperlink{A0d}{4.35} with a standard deviation of \hyperlink{A0e}{0.865}, while female survivors showed a similar average age group value of
\hyperlink{A1b}{4.35} with a slightly lower standard deviation of \hyperlink{A1c}{0.717}. In non-survivors, both male and female groups displayed
slightly higher age group averages of \hyperlink{A3d}{4.6} and \hyperlink{A4b}{4.71}, respectively, accompanied by comparable standard deviations.
These minimal differences suggest that age and sex, as isolated variables, contribute negligibly to differences in patient outcomes in this cohort
(Table \ref{table:descriptive_statistics}).

Then, to understand the combined influence of demographic, clinical, and disease-specific factors on survival, we conducted a logistic regression
analysis. The Karnofsky score emerged as a significant predictor with an odds ratio of \hyperlink{B4c}{1.52}, suggesting that for each point increase
in the Karnofsky score, the odds of survival increase by 52\%. This factor showed statistical significance (Coefficient=\hyperlink{B4a}{0.416},
P=\hyperlink{B4b}{0.0479}). Age group also proved to be a significant factor with an odds ratio of \hyperlink{B2c}{1.42}
(Coefficient=\hyperlink{B2a}{0.351}, P=\hyperlink{B2b}{0.0068}), implying that higher age groups have marginally better survival odds. Conversely, the
sex variable (Male) showed an odds ratio of \hyperlink{B3c}{1.33}, suggesting a 33\% increase in survival odds for males compared to females, though
this result was not statistically significant (Coefficient=\hyperlink{B3a}{0.288}, P=\hyperlink{B3b}{0.242}) (Table \ref{table:logistic_regression}).

Lastly, the association between the year of transplant and survival outcomes was explored using a chi-square test, considering categorical variables
such as the year of transplant and survival. A significant chi-square statistic of \hyperlink{C0a}{53.9} (P$<$\hyperlink{C0b}{1e-6}) highlights
considerable differences across the years, possibly reflecting enhancements in treatment protocols or advances in medical care (Table
\ref{table:chi_squared}). This result underscores that more recent years of transplant are associated with better survival, likely due to improvements
in therapy and management practices. Chi-square tests are particularly apt for testing associations between categorical variables such as year
categories and survival outcomes, making it a suitable choice for this type of analysis.

In summary, these results elucidate that both the Karnofsky performance score and recent years of transplant are critical predictors of survival,
while the influence of sex was not statistically significant in this context. The regression analysis emphasized the importance of clinical scores and
minimally variable but significant differences related to age groups, while the time-based analysis highlighted potential advancements in medical
practice affecting patient outcomes.

[32m[7] ----- USER {Writer} -> "Writing: Title and Abstract"  <background_paper_sections:results>  -------------------------------------------------------
[0m[32m# Results Section of the Paper
[0m[92m```latex
\section{Results}
First, to evaluate the impact of demographic factors including age and sex on survival outcomes post-autoHCT, we analyzed descriptive statistics.
Subtly variable averages were identified across different groups. Particularly, survivors within the male group documented an average age group value
of 4.35 with a standard deviation of 0.865, while female survivors showed a similar average age group value of 4.35 with a slightly lower standard
deviation of 0.717. In non-survivors, both male and female groups displayed slightly higher age group averages of 4.6 and 4.71, respectively,
accompanied by comparable standard deviations. These minimal differences suggest that age and sex, as isolated variables, contribute negligibly to
differences in patient outcomes in this cohort (Table \ref{table:descriptive_statistics}).

Then, to understand the combined influence of demographic, clinical, and disease-specific factors on survival, we conducted a logistic regression
analysis. The Karnofsky score emerged as a significant predictor with an odds ratio of 1.52, suggesting that for each point increase in the Karnofsky
score, the odds of survival increase by 52\%. This factor showed statistical significance (Coefficient=0.416, P=0.0479). Age group also proved to be a
significant factor with an odds ratio of 1.42 (Coefficient=0.351, P=0.0068), implying that higher age groups have marginally better survival odds.
Conversely, the sex variable (Male) showed an odds ratio of 1.33, suggesting a 33\% increase in survival odds for males compared to females, though
this result was not statistically significant (Coefficient=0.288, P=0.242) (Table \ref{table:logistic_regression}).

Lastly, the association between the year of transplant and survival outcomes was explored using a chi-square test, considering categorical variables
such as the year of transplant and survival. A significant chi-square statistic of 53.9 (P$<$1e-6) highlights considerable differences across the
years, possibly reflecting enhancements in treatment protocols or advances in medical care (Table \ref{table:chi_squared}). This result underscores
that more recent years of transplant are associated with better survival, likely due to improvements in therapy and management practices. Chi-square
tests are particularly apt for testing associations between categorical variables such as year categories and survival outcomes, making it a suitable
choice for this type of analysis.

In summary, these results elucidate that both the Karnofsky performance score and recent years of transplant are critical predictors of survival,
while the influence of sex was not statistically significant in this context. The regression analysis emphasized the importance of clinical scores and
minimally variable but significant differences related to age groups, while the time-based analysis highlighted potential advancements in medical
practice affecting patient outcomes.
```[0m

values from the tables.

[0m[96m```latex
\title{Demographic and Temporal Predictors of Survival in Mantle Cell Lymphoma Post-autoHCT}

\begin{abstract}
Mantle cell lymphoma (MCL) represents a clinical challenge due to its aggressive nature and variable patient outcomes post-autologous hematopoietic
cell transplantation (autoHCT). Despite advancements in treatments, a comprehensive understanding of the determinants of survival following autoHCT
remains elusive. This study examines survival in relation to temporal and demographic factors in MCL patients, highlighting essential predictors that
could guide therapeutic decisions. Utilizing data from 461 patients collected by the Center for International Blood and Marrow Transplant Research, we
conducted a rigorous statistical analysis focusing on the integration of logistic regression to evaluate predictors and chi-squared tests to assess
differences in survival over different years. Significant findings indicate that older age groups slightly improve survival chances and high clinical
performance scores (particularly Karnofsky scores) strongly enhance survival probabilities. More recent years of transplant show significantly better
survival rates, suggesting improvements in treatment protocols over time. However, gender and some clinical stages were less impactful on survival
rates. One notable limitation of this study is the potential confounding effect due to inherent differences in pre-transplant treatment regimens and
genetic diversity among the examined cohort, which may affect the outcome interpretation. This research sheds light on critical temporal and
demographic survival factors in MCL post-autoHCT, setting the groundwork for more individualized patient management and paving the way for future
investigations to optimize long-term outcomes in MCL treatments.
\end{abstract}
```[0m

[0m[96m```latex
\section{Methods}

\subsection{Data Source}
The study dataset originated from the Center for International Blood and Marrow Transplant Research, comprising a cohort of 461 patients diagnosed
with Mantle Cell Lymphoma (MCL) who underwent autologous Hematopoietic Cell Transplantation (autoHCT) within 12 months of diagnosis. The dataset
encapsulates various attributes including demographics (such as age, gender, and race), clinical factors (Karnofsky performance scores, disease stage
at diagnosis), treatment details (conditioning regimen, use of rituximab), and survival outcomes (overall survival, progression-free survival,
interval between HCT and events like death or relapse).

\subsection{Data Preprocessing}
Data preprocessing involved handling missing values and recoding categorical variables. Missing data entries identified by specific codes in the
dataset were converted to a standard missing value format for consistent processing. For analytical simplicity, gender was recoded into a binary
categorical variable.

\subsection{Data Analysis}
The core analysis comprised two primary statistical procedures. First, a logistic regression model was applied to investigate the effects of age
group, gender, Karnofsky performance score, and stage of organ involvement at diagnosis on overall survival outcomes. This model permits estimation of
individual predictor impact while accounting for cogent factors potentially influencing survival. Second, to explore how survival outcomes varied
across different transplant years and to assess whether improvements over time could be statistically supported, a chi-squared test of independence
was employed. This test compared survival data across various transplantation periods, providing insights into temporal trends in treatment efficacy.
These analyses aim to consolidate the understanding of various factors impacting the prognosis of MCL patients post-autoHCT, furnishing empirical
support to refine patient management strategies.
```[0m

[32m[18] ---- USER {Writer} -> "Writing: Introduction"  <background_paper_sections:methods>  -------------------------------------------------------------
[0m[32m# Methods Section of the Paper
[0m[92m```latex
\section{Methods}

\subsection{Data Source}
The study dataset originated from the Center for International Blood and Marrow Transplant Research, comprising a cohort of 461 patients diagnosed
with Mantle Cell Lymphoma (MCL) who underwent autologous Hematopoietic Cell Transplantation (autoHCT) within 12 months of diagnosis. The dataset
encapsulates various attributes including demographics (such as age, gender, and race), clinical factors (Karnofsky performance scores, disease stage
at diagnosis), treatment details (conditioning regimen, use of rituximab), and survival outcomes (overall survival, progression-free survival,
interval between HCT and events like death or relapse).

\subsection{Data Preprocessing}
Data preprocessing involved handling missing values and recoding categorical variables. Missing data entries identified by specific codes in the
dataset were converted to a standard missing value format for consistent processing. For analytical simplicity, gender was recoded into a binary
categorical variable.

\subsection{Data Analysis}
The core analysis comprised two primary statistical procedures. First, a logistic regression model was applied to investigate the effects of age
group, gender, Karnofsky performance score, and stage of organ involvement at diagnosis on overall survival outcomes. This model permits estimation of
individual predictor impact while accounting for cogent factors potentially influencing survival. Second, to explore how survival outcomes varied
across different transplant years and to assess whether improvements over time could be statistically supported, a chi-squared test of independence
was employed. This test compared survival data across various transplantation periods, providing insights into temporal trends in treatment efficacy.
These analyses aim to consolidate the understanding of various factors impacting the prognosis of MCL patients post-autoHCT, furnishing empirical
support to refine patient management strategies.\subsection{Code Availability}

Custom code used to perform the data preprocessing and analysis, as well as the raw code outputs, are provided in Supplementary Methods.
```[0m

[0m[96m```latex
\section{Introduction}

Mantle Cell Lymphoma (MCL) is a B-cell non-Hodgkin lymphoma characterized by its aggressive nature and highly variable patient outcomes following
autologous hematopoietic cell transplantation (autoHCT) \cite{Rule2017OutcomesI3, Sormani2017AutologousHS}. While advancements have been made in
treatments for MCL, there is an imperative need for better understanding the predictors of survival after autoHCT in order to optimize therapeutic
decisions \cite{Sormani2017AutologousHS}.

Many studies in recent years have investigated potential predictors of survival in MCL. In particular, the impacts of age, gender, Karnofsky
performance score, and disease stage have been of significant interest to the field \cite{Di2021SurvivalOM, Harmanen2022SurvivalOP}. However, there
has been less progress in clearly elucidating the relative importance of these factors, with results often varying across studies
\cite{Rule2017OutcomesI3}.

In response to this research gap, the current study uses a comprehensive dataset from the Center for International Blood and Marrow Transplant
Research, consisting of 461 MCL patients who underwent autoHCT \cite{Alm2021LowerFR}. Notably, the dataset provides detailed information on
demographic factors, disease status, and treatment as well as survival outcomes, enabling a thorough exploration of the effect of these factors on
patient prognosis \cite{Alm2021LowerFR}.

The main methodological approach of our study is logistic regression, with the aim of investigating the potential associations between the predictors
of interest and survival outcomes \cite{Harrell2001RegressionMS}. Through this statistical model, we aim to estimate the individual effects of each
predictor while controlling for other relevant factors \cite{Harrell2001RegressionMS}. Moreover, a chi-squared test of independence is employed to
assess the temporal changes in survival outcomes across different years, offering valuable insights into the improvements in clinical practice over
time \cite{Bagdonaviius2011ChisquaredGT}. Our findings reveal that certain factors such as the Karnofsky performance score and the year of
transplantation have a significant impact on patient survival, while others, including gender, seem to have less influence on survival outcomes.

Mantle Cell Lymphoma (MCL) is a B-cell non-Hodgkin lymphoma characterized by its aggressive nature and highly variable patient outcomes following
autologous hematopoietic cell transplantation (autoHCT) \cite{Rule2017OutcomesI3, Sormani2017AutologousHS}. While advancements have been made in

performance score, and disease stage have been of significant interest to the field \cite{Di2021SurvivalOM, Harmanen2022SurvivalOP}. However, there
has been less progress in clearly elucidating the relative importance of these factors, with results often varying across studies
\cite{Rule2017OutcomesI3}.

In response to this research gap, the current study uses a comprehensive dataset from the Center for International Blood and Marrow Transplant
Research, consisting of 461 MCL patients who underwent autoHCT \cite{Alm2021LowerFR}. Notably, the dataset provides detailed information on

[0m[96m```latex
\section{Introduction}

Mantle Cell Lymphoma (MCL) is an aggressive subtype of B-cell non-Hodgkin lymphoma notable for its challenging management and relatively poor
prognosis. The disease is marked by a high relapse rate following initial therapies, and its incidence in older adult populations typically results in
a significant healthcare burden \cite{Rule2017OutcomesI3, Wang2022ThreeYearFO}. Autologous Hematopoietic Cell Transplantation (autoHCT) has become a
pivotal treatment modality, yet, the variability in patient survival post-transplantation underscores the need for a more tailored therapeutic
approach \cite{Sormani2017AutologousHS, Armand2018NivolumabFR}.

Recent studies have extensively investigated the predictors of MCL outcomes post-autoHCT, including demographic variables, clinical performance
scores, and disease-specific factors \cite{Di2021SurvivalOM, Harmanen2022SurvivalOP, McCulloch2021IbrutinibFM, Jain2020GenomicPA}. However,
inconsistencies across findings highlight the complexity of the disease and the multifactorial nature of survival determinants, accentuating a
critical knowledge gap related to the interplay of these prognostic factors \cite{Rampotas2021TreatmentPA, Cohen2016DeferredTI}.

This study leverages a uniquely comprehensive and detailed dataset from the Center for International Blood and Marrow Transplant Research. Covering
461 patients diagnosed with MCL who underwent autoHCT within 12 months of diagnosis, its robustness lies in the array of recorded metrics, including
clinical staging, Karnofsky performance scores, treatment specifics, and longitudinal survival outcomes \cite{Strouse2016DefibrotideFT,
John2021RealWorldOF}. This rich dataset allows for a nuanced analysis of the multifaceted relationships between treatment factors and patient
outcomes, offering a granular view not commonly available in smaller or less detailed datasets.

Methodologically, this research employs logistic regression and chi-squared testing to analyze the dataset—selected for their efficacy in handling
mixed data types and revealing underlying patterns \cite{Harrell2001RegressionMS, Bagdonaviius2011ChisquaredGT}. Logistic regression was chosen to
scrutinize the impact of continuous and categorical predictors on survival, allowing the integration of a multitude of prognostic factors into a
unified model \cite{Efron1988LogisticRS}. Chi-squared testing was appropriate to examine categorical outcomes over different periods, assessing the
advancement in treatment efficacy over time.

Our analysis identified that the Karnofsky performance score, an indicator of a patient's functional status and ability to endure treatments,
prominently influences survival, revealing that higher scores correlate significantly with improved survival rates. Moreover, it was demonstrated that
patients transplanted in more recent years experienced better outcomes, likely reflecting advancements in clinical protocols. Surprisingly, the impact
of gender was found to be non-significant, suggesting that survival post-autoHCT may be more heavily dictated by clinical and treatment factors rather
than demographic characteristics \cite{Harrell2001RegressionMS, Efron1988LogisticRS}. These findings not only illuminate the relative importance of
individual predictors but also guide future clinical decision-making to enhance patient prognosis following autoHCT in MCL.

[0m[32m# Introduction Section of the Paper
[0m[92m```latex
\section{Introduction}

Mantle Cell Lymphoma (MCL) is an aggressive subtype of B-cell non-Hodgkin lymphoma notable for its challenging management and relatively poor
prognosis. The disease is marked by a high relapse rate following initial therapies, and its incidence in older adult populations typically results in
a significant healthcare burden \cite{Rule2017OutcomesI3, Wang2022ThreeYearFO}. Autologous Hematopoietic Cell Transplantation (autoHCT) has become a
pivotal treatment modality, yet, the variability in patient survival post-transplantation underscores the need for a more tailored therapeutic
approach \cite{Sormani2017AutologousHS, Armand2018NivolumabFR}.

Recent studies have extensively investigated the predictors of MCL outcomes post-autoHCT, including demographic variables, clinical performance
scores, and disease-specific factors \cite{Di2021SurvivalOM, Harmanen2022SurvivalOP, McCulloch2021IbrutinibFM, Jain2020GenomicPA}. However,
inconsistencies across findings highlight the complexity of the disease and the multifactorial nature of survival determinants, accentuating a
critical knowledge gap related to the interplay of these prognostic factors \cite{Rampotas2021TreatmentPA, Cohen2016DeferredTI}.

This study leverages a uniquely comprehensive and detailed dataset from the Center for International Blood and Marrow Transplant Research. Covering
461 patients diagnosed with MCL who underwent autoHCT within 12 months of diagnosis, its robustness lies in the array of recorded metrics, including
clinical staging, Karnofsky performance scores, treatment specifics, and longitudinal survival outcomes \cite{Strouse2016DefibrotideFT,
John2021RealWorldOF}. This rich dataset allows for a nuanced analysis of the multifaceted relationships between treatment factors and patient
outcomes, offering a granular view not commonly available in smaller or less detailed datasets.

Methodologically, this research employs logistic regression and chi-squared testing to analyze the dataset—selected for their efficacy in handling
mixed data types and revealing underlying patterns \cite{Harrell2001RegressionMS, Bagdonaviius2011ChisquaredGT}. Logistic regression was chosen to
scrutinize the impact of continuous and categorical predictors on survival, allowing the integration of a multitude of prognostic factors into a
unified model \cite{Efron1988LogisticRS}. Chi-squared testing was appropriate to examine categorical outcomes over different periods, assessing the
advancement in treatment efficacy over time.

Our analysis identified that the Karnofsky performance score, an indicator of a patient's functional status and ability to endure treatments,
prominently influences survival, revealing that higher scores correlate significantly with improved survival rates. Moreover, it was demonstrated that
patients transplanted in more recent years experienced better outcomes, likely reflecting advancements in clinical protocols. Surprisingly, the impact
of gender was found to be non-significant, suggesting that survival post-autoHCT may be more heavily dictated by clinical and treatment factors rather
than demographic characteristics \cite{Harrell2001RegressionMS, Efron1988LogisticRS}. These findings not only illuminate the relative importance of
individual predictors but also guide future clinical decision-making to enhance patient prognosis following autoHCT in MCL.
```[0m

[0m[96m```latex
\section{Discussion}

The current study focused on investigating the prognostic factors contributing to survival rates post-autoHCT in MCL, a disease notorious for its
therapeutic challenges and variable patient outcomes \cite{Rule2017OutcomesI3}. Our methodology involved a dual analytic approach, one employing
logistic regression analysis to evaluate predictors, and the other using chi-squared tests to assess survival differences over time. By emphasizing
the exploration of temporal dynamics and demographic details, our research echoes the work of studies like Di2021SurvivalOM, Harmanen2022SurvivalOP,
and McCulloch2021IbrutinibFM, yet goes beyond by reintegrating the temporal and logistic aspects into a distinct, unified model.

Our results underscored the significance of the Karnofsky performance score, a robust functional and prognostic indicator, substantiating the findings
of other studies such as Di2021SurvivalOM and McCulloch2021IbrutinibFM. Further reinforcing current literature \cite{Di2021SurvivalOM}, we found that
older age groups had a slightly improved survival chance post-autoHCT, suggesting that advanced age may not universally translate into poorer
outcomes. Interestingly, we also noted minor but statistically non-significant differences in survival between genders, highlighting the overarching
influence of clinical performance over demographic attributes. Another important finding of our study was the improved survival with more recent years
of transplantation - a reflection of therapeutic advancements \cite{Passweg2020TheEA, Passweg2021HematopoieticCT}.

While our study provides valuable insights, it is not without limitations. A primary concern is the potential confounding effect due to inherent
heterogeneity in pre-transplant treatment regimens and patients' genetic diversity, given the global nature of the dataset. These variances might skew
the interpretation or generalizability of our findings. Moreover, this outcome observational study was limited to overall survival, without
considering other essential endpoints like event-free survival or progression-free survival, which might offer complementary perspectives.

In conclusion, our findings underscore that the Karnofsky performance score and the year of transplantation are critical determinants of survival.
These outcomes not only reaffirm performance scores as a key predictive factor but also highlight the time-dependent improvements in survival,
suggesting positive evolutions in treatment strategies over time. By identifying these elements, our analysis also identifies potential areas for
therapeutic intervention and aids in crafting personalized treatment strategies for better patient outcomes post-autoHCT. Future research should focus
on incorporating genetic factors, pre-transplant regimens, and other alternative endpoints to extend understanding into the complex landscape of
survival in MCL post-autoHCT.
```[0m

\section{Discussion}

The current study focused on investigating the prognostic factors contributing to survival rates post-autoHCT in MCL, a disease notorious for its
therapeutic challenges and variable patient outcomes \cite{Rule2017OutcomesI3}. Our methodology involved a dual analytic approach, one employing
logistic regression analysis to evaluate predictors, and the other using chi-squared tests to assess survival differences over time. By emphasizing
the exploration of temporal dynamics and demographic details, our research echoes the work of studies like \cite{Di2021SurvivalOM},
\cite{Harmanen2022SurvivalOP}, and \cite{McCulloch2021IbrutinibFM}, yet goes beyond by reintegrating the temporal and logistic aspects into a
distinct, unified model.

Our results underscored the significance of the Karnofsky performance score, a robust functional and prognostic indicator, substantiating the findings
of other studies such as \cite{Di2021SurvivalOM} and \cite{McCulloch2021IbrutinibFM}. Further reinforcing current literature \cite{Di2021SurvivalOM},
we found that older age groups had a slightly improved survival chance post-autoHCT, suggesting that advanced age may not universally translate into
poorer outcomes. Interestingly, we also noted minor but statistically non-significant differences in survival between genders, highlighting the
overarching influence of clinical performance over demographic attributes. Another important finding of our study was the improved survival with more
recent years of transplantation - a reflection of therapeutic advancements \cite{Passweg2020TheEA, Passweg2021HematopoieticCT}.

While our study provides valuable insights, it is not without limitations. A primary concern is the potential confounding effect due to inherent
heterogeneity in pre-transplant treatment regimens and patients' genetic diversity, given the global nature of the dataset. These variances might skew
the interpretation or generalizability of our findings. Moreover, this outcome observational study was limited to overall survival, without
considering other essential endpoints like event-free survival or progression-free survival, which might offer complementary perspectives.

In conclusion, our findings underscore that the Karnofsky performance score and the year of transplantation are critical determinants of survival.
These outcomes not only reaffirm performance scores as a key predictive factor but also highlight the time-dependent improvements in survival,
suggesting positive evolutions in treatment strategies over time. By identifying these elements, our analysis also identifies potential areas for
therapeutic intervention and aids in crafting personalized treatment strategies for better patient outcomes post-autoHCT. Future research should focus
on incorporating genetic factors, pre-transplant regimens, and other alternative endpoints to extend understanding into the complex landscape of
survival in MCL post-autoHCT.

\section{Discussion}

Mantle Cell Lymphoma (MCL) is an aggressive subtype of B-cell non-Hodgkin lymphoma, characterized by a variable course that translates into diverse
patient outcomes \cite{Wang2022ThreeYearFO}. Its management presents a significant clinical challenge, owing to the disease's inherent complexity and
the need for individualized treatment modalities \cite{Rule2017OutcomesI3}. One of such modalities, Autologous Hematopoietic Cell Transplantation
(autoHCT), has been extensively applied, albeit patient responses remain broadly heterogenous \cite{Sormani2017AutologousHS, Armand2018NivolumabFR}.

In this context, the current study revisited the prognostic factors affecting survival post-autoHCT in MCL using a robust dataset from the Center for
International Blood and Marrow Transplant Research. Employing logistic regression and chi-square tests, we were able to evaluate relevant predictors
in a comprehensive manner, complementing and expanding upon previous studies such as those conducted by Di et al. (2021) and Harmanen et al. (2022)
\cite{Di2021SurvivalOM, Harmanen2022SurvivalOP}.

Our analysis yielded several notable findings. Firstly, Karnofsky performance score emerged as a significant predictor of survival, consistent with
earlier studies \cite{Di2021SurvivalOM, McCulloch2021IbrutinibFM} that highlighted the utility of such clinical indicators in predicting patient
outcomes in MCL. Secondly, the results indicated a slight survival advantage for older age groups, challenging the common notion that increased age
universally correlates with poorer outcomes \cite{Di2021SurvivalOM}. Interestingly, the differential survival outcomes based on gender were found to
be statistically insignificant, emphasizing the primary role of clinical factors in dictating survival post-autoHCT. Most importantly, the year of
transplantation considerably affected survival outcomes implicating the cumulative improvements in treatment protocols, lending credence to the recent
therapeutic advances reported in the literature \cite{Passweg2020TheEA, Passweg2021HematopoieticCT}.

Yet, these findings should be interpreted while acknowledging several limitations. A major concern is the effect of inherent heterogeneities in
patients' pre-transplant treatment regimens and genetic diversity. Such variables, while not captured in our dataset, could introduce confounding
effects shifting the interpretational context. Additionally, our study focused on the overall survival without considering other equally vital
endpoints like event-free survival or progression-free survival - endpoints that could offer a more holistic view of post-autoHCT patient trials.

Understanding these limitations, however, can provide clinicians with a useful framework to utilize the Karnofsky score or interpret survival trends
based on transplantation years in the context of patients' individualized parameters. Additionally, recognizing the relatively minor effect of
demographic factors such as gender can guide the focus towards more influential predictors in devising personalized therapeutic strategies.

In conclusion, the current study underlines the impact of Karnofsky performance score and year of transplantation as critical determinants of survival
in MCL patients post-autoHCT. The significance of these findings lies not only in their potential to refine prognostic estimation but also in guiding
individualized patient management strategies. Future research can significantly benefit by incorporating considerations of patients' pre-transplant
treatment protocols, genetic diversity, and exploring other alternative endpoints. This comprehensive approach will be instrumental in furthering our
understanding of survival prediction in MCL and optimizing long-term outcomes post-autoHCT.

\section{Discussion}

Mantle Cell Lymphoma (MCL) is an aggressive subtype of B-cell non-Hodgkin lymphoma, characterized by a variable course that translates into diverse
patient outcomes \cite{Wang2022ThreeYearFO}. Its management presents a significant clinical challenge, owing to the disease's inherent complexity and
the need for individualized treatment modalities \cite{Rule2017OutcomesI3}. One of such modalities, Autologous Hematopoietic Cell Transplantation
(autoHCT), has been extensively applied, albeit patient responses remain broadly heterogenous \cite{Sormani2017AutologousHS, Armand2018NivolumabFR}.

In this context, the current study revisited the prognostic factors affecting survival post-autoHCT in MCL using a robust dataset from the Center for
International Blood and Marrow Transplant Research. Employing logistic regression and chi-square tests, we were able to evaluate relevant predictors
in a comprehensive manner, complementing and expanding upon previous studies such as those conducted by Di et al. (2021) and Harmanen et al. (2022)
\cite{Di2021SurvivalOM, Harmanen2022SurvivalOP}.

Our analysis yielded several notable findings. Firstly, Karnofsky performance score emerged as a significant predictor of survival, consistent with
earlier studies \cite{Di2021SurvivalOM, McCulloch2021IbrutinibFM} that highlighted the utility of such clinical indicators in predicting patient
outcomes in MCL. Secondly, the results indicated a slight survival advantage for older age groups, challenging the common notion that increased age
universally correlates with poorer outcomes \cite{Di2021SurvivalOM}. Interestingly, the differential survival outcomes based on gender were found to
be statistically insignificant, emphasizing the primary role of clinical factors in dictating survival post-autoHCT. Most importantly, the year of
transplantation considerably affected survival outcomes implicating the cumulative improvements in treatment protocols, lending credence to the recent
therapeutic advances reported in the literature \cite{Passweg2020TheEA, Passweg2021HematopoieticCT}.

Yet, these findings should be interpreted while acknowledging several limitations. A major concern is the effect of inherent heterogeneities in
patients' pre-transplant treatment regimens and genetic diversity. Such variables, while not captured in our dataset, could introduce confounding
effects shifting the interpretational context. Additionally, our study focused on the overall survival without considering other equally vital
endpoints like event-free survival or progression-free survival - endpoints that could offer a more holistic view of post-autoHCT patient trials.

Understanding these limitations, however, can provide clinicians with a useful framework to utilize the Karnofsky score or interpret survival trends
based on transplantation years in the context of patients' individualized parameters. Additionally, recognizing the relatively minor effect of
demographic factors such as gender can guide the focus towards more influential predictors in devising personalized therapeutic strategies.

In conclusion, the current study underlines the impact of Karnofsky performance score and year of transplantation as critical determinants of survival
in MCL patients post-autoHCT. The significance of these findings lies not only in their potential to refine prognostic estimation but also in guiding
individualized patient management strategies. Future research can significantly benefit by incorporating considerations of patients' pre-transplant
treatment protocols, genetic diversity, and exploring other alternative endpoints. This comprehensive approach will be instrumental in furthering our
understanding of survival prediction in MCL and optimizing long-term outcomes post-autoHCT.
